### [New version of â€œIntro to Brain-Like-AGI Safetyâ€](https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/rreDwHXgnhEDKxkro/new-version-of-intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety)

**æ¥æº**: LessWrong - AI

**æ‘˜è¦**: Published on January 23, 2026 4:21 PM GMT<br /><br /><p>A new version of <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/s/HzcM2dkCq7fwXBej8">â€œIntro to Brain-Like-AGI Safetyâ€</a> is out!</p><h1>Things that have not changed</h1><p><strong>Same links as before</strong>:</p><ul><li><strong>As a series of 15 blog posts on LessWrong / Alignment Forum:</strong> <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/s/HzcM2dkCq7fwXBej8">https://www.lesswrong.com/s/HzcM2dkCq7fwXBej8</a></li><li><strong>As a 225-page PDF (now up to version 3):</strong> <a href="https://osf.io/preprints/osf/fe36n">https://osf.io/preprints/osf/fe36n</a></li><li><strong>Summary video:</strong> <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/YKmyay3bWF2ofAGNo/video-and-transcript-challenges-for-safe-and-beneficial">Video &amp; transcript: Challenges for Safe &amp; Beneficial Brain-Like AGI</a></li></ul><p><strong>â€¦And same abstract as before</strong>:</p><blockquote><p>Suppose we someday build an Artificial General Intelligence algorithm using similar principles of learning and cognition as the human brain. How would we use such an algorithm safely?</p><p>I will argue that this is an open technical problem, and my goal in this post series is to bring readers with no prior knowledge all the way up to the front-line of unsolved problems as I see them.</p><p><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/4basF9w9jaPZpoC8R/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-1-what-s-the-problem-and-why">Post #1</a> contains definitions, background, and motivation. Then Posts <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/wBHSYwqssBGCnwvHg/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-2-learning-from-scratch-in">#2</a>â€“<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/zXibERtEWpKuG5XAC/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-7-from-hardcoded-drives-to">#7</a> are the neuroscience, arguing for a picture of he brain that combines large-scale learning algorithms (e.g. in the cortex) and specific evolved reflexes (e.g. in the hypothalamus and brainstem). Posts <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/fDPsYdDtkzhBp9A8D/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-8-takeaways-from-neuro-1-2-on">#8</a>â€“<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/tj8AC3vhTnBywdZoA/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-15-conclusion-open-problems-1">#15</a> apply those neuroscience ideas directly to AGI safety, ending with a list of open questions and advice for getting involved in the field.</p><p>A major theme will be that the human brain runs a yet-to-be-invented variation on Model-Based Reinforcement Learning. The reward function of this system (a.k.a. â€œinnate drivesâ€ or â€œprimary rewardsâ€) says that pain is bad, and eating-when-hungry is good, etc. I will argue that this reward function is centered around the hypothalamus and brainstem, and that <i>all</i> human desiresâ€”even â€œhigherâ€ desires for things like compassion and justiceâ€”come directly or indirectly from that reward function. If future programmers build brain-like AGI, they will likewise have a reward function slot in their source code, in which they can put whatever they want. If they put the wrong code in the reward function slot, the resulting AGI will wind up callously indifferent to human welfare. How might they avoid that? What code <i>should</i> they put inâ€”along with training environment and other design choicesâ€”such that the AGI wonâ€™t feel callous indifference to whether its programmers, and other people, live or die? No one knowsâ€”itâ€™s an open problem, but I will review some ideas and research directions.</p></blockquote><h1>Highlights from the changelog</h1><p>So whatâ€™s new? Well, I went through the whole thing and made a bunch of edits and additions, based on what Iâ€™ve (hopefully) learned since my <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/btHmC88KCZdzimBCM/steve2152-s-shortform?commentId=DD28htp2tWZg7w8eT">last big round of edits 18 months ago</a>. Here are some highlights:</p><h2><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/4basF9w9jaPZpoC8R/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-1-what-s-the-problem-and-why">Post 1: What's the problem &amp; Why work on it now?</a></h2><h3><i>What is AGI?</i></h3><p>I updated my â€œwhat is AGIâ€ chart, along with more elaboration on why some (not all!) LLM enthusiasts have a blind spot for just how much headroom there still is beyond the AI of today.</p><figure class="image image_resized" style="width: 82.42%;"><img alt="2-column table contrasting properties of AI as we think of it today, with properties of future AGI that I'm thinking about" src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/4basF9w9jaPZpoC8R/cziczguyma3nifu1x3th" /></figure><blockquote><p>â€¦</p><p>I should elaborate on that last part. I think that some LLM enthusiasts have a massive blind spot, where they are so impressed by all the things that todayâ€™s LLMs can do, that they forget about all the things that todayâ€™s LLMs canâ€™t do. These people read the questions on <a href="https://lastexam.ai/">Humanityâ€™s Last Exam</a> (HLE), and scratch their heads, and say â€œCâ€™mon, when LLMs ace the HLE benchmark, then what else is there? Look at how hard those questions are! It would need to be way beyond PhD level in everything! If thatâ€™s not superintelligence, what is?â€</p><p>Well, no, thatâ€™s not superintelligence, and hereâ€™s an example of why not. Consider the task of writing a business plan and then founding a company and growing it, over the course of years, to $1B/year revenue, all with zero human intervention. Todayâ€™s LLMs fall wildly, comically short of being able to complete that task. By analogy, if humans were like todayâ€™s AIs, then humans would be able to do some narrow bits of founding and running companies by ourselves, but we would need some intelligent non-human entity (angels?) to repeatedly intervene, assign tasks to us humans, and keep the larger project on track. Of course, humans (and groups of humans) donâ€™t need the help of angels to conceive and carry out ambitious projects, like building businesses or going to the moon. We can do it all by ourselves. So by the same token, future AGIs (and groups of AGIs) wonâ€™t need the help of humans.</p><p>Anyway, this series is about brain-like algorithms. These algorithms are by definition capable of doing absolutely every intelligent behavior that humans (and groups and societies of humans) can do, and potentially much more. So they can <i>definitely</i> reach AGI. Whereas todayâ€™s AI algorithms are <i>not</i> AGI. So somewhere in between here and there, thereâ€™s a fuzzy line that separates â€œAGIâ€ from â€œnot AGIâ€. Where exactly is that line? My answer: I donâ€™t know, and I donâ€™t care. Drawing that line has never come up for me as a useful thing to do. It wonâ€™t come up in this series either.</p></blockquote><h3><i>More responses to intelligence denialists</i></h3><p>I added my response to another flavor of â€œintelligence denialismâ€</p><blockquote><p><i><strong><u>Opinion #6:</u></strong></i> <i>â€œBrain-like AGI is kinda an incoherent concept; intelligence requires embodiment, not just a brain in a vat (or on a chip). And we get things done by trial-and-error, and cooperation across a society, not by some abstract â€˜intelligenceâ€™.â€</i></p><ul><li>The â€œembodimentâ€ debate in neuroscience continues to rage. I fall somewhere in the middle. I do think that future AGIs will have <i>some</i> action spaceâ€”e.g., the ability to (virtually) summon a particular book and open it to a particular passage. I <i>donâ€™t</i> think having a whole literal body is importantâ€”for example <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Nolan_(author)"><u>Christopher Nolan</u></a> (1965-2009) had lifelong quadriplegia, but it didnâ€™t prevent him from being an acclaimed author and poet. More importantly, I expect that whatever aspects of embodiment are important for intelligence could be easily incorporated into a brain-like AGI running on a silicon chip. Is a body necessary for intelligence after all? OK sure, we can give the AGI a virtual body in a VR worldâ€”or even a real robot body in the real world! Are hormonal signals necessary for intelligence? OK sure, we can code up some virtual hormonal signals. Etc.</li><li>As for societies and cooperation, thatâ€™s intimately tied to human intelligence. Billions of humans over thousands of years developed language and science and a $100T economy entirely from scratch. Could billions of rocks over thousands of years do all that? Nope. What about billions of todayâ€™s LLMs over thousands of years? Also nope. (Indeed, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicaraguan_Sign_Language">unlike humans</a>, LLMs cannot invent language from scratch unless they already have language in their training data.) On the other hand, if billions of humans can do all that, then so can billions of brain-like AGIs.</li><li>And separately, â€œa group of billions of AGIs that collaborate and exchange ideas using languageâ€ is â€¦ also an AGI! Itâ€™s an AGI that takes billions of times more chips to run, but itâ€™s still an AGI. Donâ€™t underestimate what one AGI can do!</li><li>Thereâ€™s a common conflation of â€œintelligenceâ€ with â€œbook smartsâ€. This is deeply mistaken. E.g. <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/aiQabnugDhcrFtr9n/the-power-of-intelligence">as the saying goes</a>, charisma resides in the brain, not the kidneys.</li></ul></blockquote><h2><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/wBHSYwqssBGCnwvHg/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-2-learning-from-scratch-in">Post 2: â€œLearning from scratchâ€ in the brain</a></h2><h3><i>Better overview of the discourse</i></h3><p>Having learned more about the range of opinions on my â€œlearning from scratchâ€ hypothesis, I wrote a better overview of the state of the discourse:</p><blockquote><p>â€¦Nevertheless, hereâ€™s a caricatured roundup of where different groups seem to stand.</p><p><strong>In favor</strong> <strong>of the learning-from-scratch hypothesis:</strong></p><ul><li><strong>Many computational neuroscientists in the old-school â€œconnectionistâ€ tradition,</strong> such as Randall Oâ€™Reilly, would probably endorse most or all of my hypothesis. At the very least, Oâ€™Reilly explicitly endorses a learning-from-scratch cortex in <a href="https://youtu.be/dMI4-xvuhNw?si=NpVQ0xd4gWxVwOy1&amp;t=1800"><u>this interview</u></a>, citing <a href="http://doi.org/10.1037/0033-295x.98.2.164"><u>Morton &amp; Johnson (1991)</u></a> as a line of evidence.</li><li><strong>Many people who talk about â€œold brain (a.k.a. lizard brain) versus new brainâ€,</strong> such as Jeff Hawkins and Dileep George, would also probably endorse most or all of my hypothesis, with the learning-from-scratch parts of the brain being â€œnewâ€ and the rest being â€œlizardâ€. More on this (slightly dubious) school of thought in <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/hE56gYi5d68uux9oM/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-3-two-subsystems-learning-and#3_3__Triune_Brain_Theory__is_wrong__but_let_s_not_throw_out_the_baby_with_the_bathwater">Â§3.3</a>.</li><li><strong>Many NeuroAI computational modelers (but not all)</strong> build their computational models of the cortex, hippocampus, cerebellum, and striatum, in a way that winds up being learning-from-scratch, I think.</li><li><strong>The book </strong><a href="https://www.amazon.com/Beyond-Evolutionary-Psychology-Neuropsychological-Modules/dp/1107661412"><i><strong>Beyond Evolutionary Psychology</strong></i><strong> by George Ellis &amp; Mark Solms (2017)</strong></a> is not quite a perfect match to my hypothesis, but itâ€™s close, probably closer than anything else Iâ€™ve seen in the literature. They divide the brain into (what they call) â€œhard-wired domainsâ€ versus â€œsoft-wired domainsâ€, with a similar boundary as mine. Kudos to the authors, even if I have some nitpicky complaints about their formulations. Too bad their book got so little attention.</li></ul><p><strong>Opposed</strong> <strong>to the learning-from-scratch hypothesis:</strong></p><ul><li><strong>People in the â€œevolved modularityâ€ / â€œinnate cognitive modulesâ€ tradition</strong> would probably strongly disagree that the cortex â€œlearns from scratchâ€. This category includes most evolutionary psychologists (e.g. Leda Cosmides, John Tooby, Steven Pinker), as well as Joshua Tenenbaum and others in the probabilistic programming school of computational cognitive science. See for example Pinkerâ€™s <a href="https://www.amazon.com/Blank-Slate-Modern-Denial-Nature/dp/0142003344"><i>The Blank Slate</i> (2003)</a> chapter 5. To be clear, Iâ€™m enthusiastic about evolutionary psychology as a topic of study, but I do think that evolutionary psychology <i>as practiced today</i> is built on a mistaken framework.</li><li><strong>People who use phrases like â€œevolutionary pretrainingâ€</strong> are likewise rejecting the learning-from-scratch framework. â€œEvolutionary pretrainingâ€ invokes the idea that the cortex is like a machine learning (ML) algorithm, but not an ML algorithm that starts from random initialization, but rather a â€œpretrainedâ€ ML algorithm that can perform useful innate behaviors from the start, thanks to evolution, in a fashion analogous to <a href="https://aws.amazon.com/what-is/transfer-learning/">â€œtransfer learningâ€</a> in the ML literature. I obviously disagree.</li><li><strong>People who talk about learning versus innateness in a way that lumps the whole brain together</strong> are implicitly rejecting the idea of learning-from-scratchâ€”or more likely, failing to consider it in the first place. Examples include the paper&nbsp;<a href="https://www.nature.com/articles/s41593-019-0520-2">â€œA deep learning framework for neuroscienceâ€ (2019, Blake Richards + 31 more coauthors)</a>, and <a href="https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-019-11786-6">â€œA critique of pure learning and what artificial neural networks can learn from animal brainsâ€ (Tony Zador, 2019)</a>, neither of which even mentions the possibility that maybe the cortex is wildly different from the brainstem when it comes to learning.</li></ul></blockquote><h3><i>Plasticity</i></h3><p>I revamped my discussion of brain plasticity, relating it to â€œmutable variablesâ€ in computer science:</p><blockquote><p>2.3.3 Learning-from-scratch is NOT the more general notion of â€œplasticityâ€</p><p>When lay people talk about â€œbrain plasticityâ€ in self-help books, theyâ€™re usually imagining a kind of animistic force that makes your brain magically respond to changing circumstances in good ways rather than bad.</p><p>Neuroscientists think of it differently: â€œplasticityâ€ means thereâ€™s something in the brain (often synapses, but also potentially myelination, gene expression, etc.) that durably changes in a specific way under specific conditions, as a result of some biochemical triggering mechanism. The change does what it does, and its consequences can be good or bad (but itâ€™s usually good, or else the triggering mechanism would not have evolved).</p><p>If we now switch over from biochemistry to algorithms, the previous paragraph may ring a bell: plasticity just means that the brain algorithm has <strong>mutable variables</strong>.</p><p>Now, any learning-from-scratch algorithm necessarily involves mutable variables, which allow it to learn. For example, the â€œweightsâ€ of a deep neural network are mutable variables.</p><p>However, practically every other algorithm involves mutable variables too! For example, the Python code <code>a=a+1</code> takes a mutable variable <code>a</code>, and increases its value by 1. When you play a video game, there are mutable variables tracking your inventory, health, progress, etc. Mutable variables are ubiquitous and routine.</p><p>The brain does other things too, besides learning-from-scratch algorithms (more on which in the <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/hE56gYi5d68uux9oM/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-3-two-subsystems-learning-and">next post</a>). Those other things, of course, also involve mutable variables, and hence plasticity. Thus, there is brain plasticity that has nothing to do with learning-from-scratch algorithms. Letâ€™s call those other things â€œidiosyncratic mutable variablesâ€. Hereâ€™s a table with an example of each, and general ways in which they differâ€¦</p></blockquote><h3><i>Interpretability</i></h3><p>Iâ€™ve talked to a number of people who think that interpretability is a silver bullet for brain-like-AGI safety, so I added this subsection in response:</p><blockquote><p>2.7.1 What about interpretability for brain-like AGIs?</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>There is a field of â€œmachine learning interpretabilityâ€, dedicated to understanding the innards of learned-from-scratch â€œtrained modelsâ€. Iâ€™m generally in favor of efforts to advance that field, but I want to caution strongly against people pinning their hopes on interpretability. Sure, given enough time, maybe people could understand any given entry in the giant unlabeled world-model of a brain-like AGI. But (1) Also, maybe not (e.g., think of the thousands of implicit hard-to-describe learned heuristics used by a charismatic salesperson); (2) If it takes careful scrutiny to understand one entry, how will we deal with millions of them? (3) A brain-like AGI would keep learning and changing as it runsâ€”do we have to keep pausing it to reanalyze its ever-shifting world-model and desires? (4) And if we keep pausing it, wonâ€™t we get outcompeted by the more careless firm down the block that just lets its AGI run at full speed?</p><p>Relatedly, people sometimes argue that the unlabeled world-model of a brain-like AGI would be easier to understand than the unlabeled world-model of an LLM, for various reasons. Whether this is true or false, I think itâ€™s a red herring. The above four issues would need to be dealt with, regardless of whether or not LLMs have an <i>even worse</i> interpretability profile than brain-like AGI.</p><p>To be clear, Iâ€™m pushing back on the idea that â€œwe donâ€™t have to worry about safety because interpretability is tractableâ€. Iâ€™m not opposed to interpretability itself, nor to leveraging interpretability as <i>one component</i> of a plan. More on the latter later in the series (esp. <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/QpHewJvZJFaQYuLwH/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-14-controlled-agi">Post #14</a>).</p></blockquote><h2><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/hE56gYi5d68uux9oM/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-3-two-subsystems-learning-and">Post 3: Two subsystems: Learning &amp; Steering</a></h2><h3><i>My timelines prediction</i></h3><p>I added my extraordinarily bold prediction on when brain-like AGI will appear. In case youâ€™re wondering, yes I am willing to put my money where my mouth is and take bets on â€œbetween zero and infinity yearsâ€, at 1:1 odds&nbsp;ğŸ˜‰</p><blockquote><p>â€¦Thus concludes my timeline-to-brain-like-AGI discussion, which again is not my main focus in this series. Or I guess maybe more than 25 years, who knows. Or I guess maybe less than 5 years, who knows. Shrug.</i></p></blockquote><h3><i>Responses to bad takes on acting under uncertainty</i></h3><p>I rewrote my round-up of bad takes on AGI uncertainty, including â€œthe insane bizarro-world reversal of Pascalâ€™s Wagerâ€:</p><blockquote><p>â€¦Likewise, the word â€œhypotheticalâ€, as in â€œhypothetical possibilitiesâ€ or â€œhypothetical risksâ€, is another jeer thrown at people trying to plan for an uncertain future. Taken literally, itâ€™s a rather odd insult: some hypotheses are false, but also, some hypotheses are true!</p><p>Various other bad takes revolve around the idea that we should not even try to prepare for possible-but-uncertain future events. In particular, some people talk as if we shouldnâ€™t try to mitigate a possible future catastrophe until weâ€™re &gt;99.9% confident that the catastrophe will definitely happen. I like to call this <strong>the insane bizarro-world reversal of Pascalâ€™s Wager</strong>. It probably sounds like a strawman when I put it like that, but my goodness, it is very real, and very widespread. brainstem</i></h3><p>I added a brief summary of why we should believe that desires like friendship and justice come ultimately from little hypothalamic cell groups, just like hunger and pain do, as opposed to purely from â€œreasonâ€ (as one high-level AI safety funder once confidently told me).</p><blockquote><p>Here are four views on why we should believe that the Steering Subsystem is the ultimate source of not only ego-dystonic urges like hunger, but also ego-syntonic desires like friendship and justice.</p><ul><li><i>AI perspective:</i> We donâ€™t yet know <i>in full detail</i> how model-based RL and model-based planning works in the human brainâ€”we donâ€™t have brain-like AGI yet. But we do at least vaguely know how these kinds of algorithms work. And we know enough to say for sure that these algorithms donâ€™t develop prosocial motivations out of nowhere. For example, if you set the reward function of MuZero to always return 0, then the algorithm will emit random outputs foreverâ€”it wonâ€™t start fighting for justice.</li><li><i>Rodent model perspective:</i> For what itâ€™s worth, researchers have been equally successful in finding little cell groups in the rodent hypothalamus that orchestrate â€œantisocialâ€ behaviors like aggression, and that orchestrate â€œprosocialâ€ behaviors like parenting and sociality. I fully expect that the same holds for humans.</li><li><i>Philosophy perspective:</i> Without the Steering Subsystem, the only thing the cortex can do is build a world-model from predictive learning of sensory inputs (<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/Y3bkJ59j4dciiLYyw/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-4-the-short-term-predictor#4_7__Short_term_predictor__example__2__Predictive_learning_of_sensory_inputs_in_the_cortex">Â§4.7</a>). Thatâ€™s â€œisâ€, not â€œoughtâ€. And <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Is%E2%80%93ought_problem">â€œHumeâ€™s lawâ€</a> says that you canâ€™t get â€œoughtâ€-statements from exclusively â€œisâ€-statements. Granted, not everyone believes in Humeâ€™s law. But I doâ€”see an elegant and concise argument for it&nbsp;<a href="https://intelligence.org/2018/02/28/sam-harris-and-eliezer-yudkowsky/#:~:text=The%20question%20%E2%80%9CHow%20many%20paperclips%20result%20if%20I%20follow%20this%20policy%3F%E2%80%9D%20is%20an%20%E2%80%9Cis%E2%80%9D%20question.">here</a>.</li><li><i>My nuts-and-bolts proposals:</i> If you keep reading this series, along with follow-up work <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/kYvbHCDeMTCTE9TAj/neuroscience-of-human-social-instincts-a-sketch">Neuroscience of human social instincts: a sketch (2024)</a>, and <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/KuBiv9cCbZ6ALjHFw/social-drives-1-sympathy-reward-from-compassion-to">Social drives 1: â€œSympathy Rewardâ€, from compassion to dehumanization (2025)</a>, and <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/fPxgFHfs5yHzYqgG7/social-drives-2-approval-reward-from-norm-enforcement-to">Social drives 2: â€œApproval Rewardâ€, from norm-enforcement to status-seeking (2025)</a>, I claim to have a rather detailed step-by-step picture of how little cell groups in the Steering Subsystem ultimately lead to humans caring deeply about friendship, justice, and so on.</li></ul></blockquote><h2><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/wucncPjud27mLWZzQ/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-10-the-technical-alignment">Post 10: The technical alignment problem</a></h2><h3><i>LLMs</i></h3><p>LLMs are officially off-topic, but in order to keep up with the times, I keep having to mention them in more and more places. One of the changes this time was to add a subsection â€œDidnâ€™t LLMs solve the Goodhartâ€™s Law problem?â€</p><blockquote><p>10.3.1.1 Didnâ€™t LLMs solve the Goodhartâ€™s Law problem?</p><p>When I first published this series in early 2022, I didnâ€™t really need to defend the idea that Goodhartâ€™s Law is an AI failure mode, because it was ubiquitous and obvious. We want AIs that find innovative, out-of-the-box solutions to problems (like winning at chess or making money), and thatâ€™s how we build AIs, so of course thatâ€™s what they do. And there isnâ€™t a principled distinction between â€œmeet the spec via a clever solution that I wouldnâ€™t have thought ofâ€, versus â€œmeet the spec by exploiting a loophole in how the spec is operationalizedâ€. You canâ€™t get the former without the latter. This is obvious in theory, and equally obvious in practice. Everyone in AI had seen it with their own eyes.</p><p>â€¦But then LLM chatbots came along, and gave rise to the popular idea that â€œAI that obeys instructions in a common-sense wayâ€ is a solved problem. Ask Claude Code to ensure that <code>render.cpp</code> has no memory leaks, and it wonâ€™t technically â€œsatisfyâ€ the â€œspecâ€ by deleting all of the code from the file. Well, actually, it might sometimes; I donâ€™t want to downplay LLM alignment challenges. See Soares &amp; Yudkowsky FAQ, <a href="https://ifanyonebuildsit.com/4/arent-developers-regularly-making-their-ais-nice-and-safe-and-obedient">â€œArenâ€™t developers regularly making their AIs nice and safe and obedient?â€</a> for a pessimistâ€™s case.</p><p>But more importantly, regardless of whether itâ€™s a solved problem for todayâ€™s LLMs, it is <i>definitely not</i> a solved problem for brain-like AGI, nor for the more general category of â€œRL agentsâ€. They are profoundly different algorithm classes. You canâ€™t extrapolate from one to the other.</p><p>See my post <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/bnnKGSCHJghAvqPjS/foom-and-doom-2-technical-alignment-is-hard">Foom &amp; Doom&nbsp;2: Technical alignment is hard (2025)</a> for a deep-dive into how people get LLMs to (more-or-less) follow common-sense instructions without egregious scheming and deception, and why we wonâ€™t be able to just use that same trick for brain-like AGI. The short version is that LLM capabilities come overwhelmingly from imitation learning. â€œTrueâ€ imitation learning is weird, and profoundly different from everyday human social imitation, or indeed anything else in biology; I like to call it â€œthe magical transmutation of observations into behaviorâ€ to emphasize its weirdness. Anyway, I claim that true imitation learning has alignment benefits (LLMs can arguably obey common-sense instructions), but those benefits are inexorably yoked to capabilities costs (LLMs canâ€™t really â€œfigure things outâ€ beyond the human distribution, the way that societies of humans can, as I discuss in <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/2yLyT6kB7BQvTfEuZ/sharp-left-turn-discourse-an-opinionated-review">my â€œSharp Left Turnâ€ post (2025)</a>). So itâ€™s a trick that wonâ€™t help us with brain-like AGI. Again, more details in <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/bnnKGSCHJghAvqPjS/foom-and-doom-2-technical-alignment-is-hard">that link</a>.</p></blockquote><h3><i>Instrumental convergence &amp; consequentialist preferences</i></h3><p>I rewrote my discussion of instrumental convergence, its relation to consequentialist preferences, and the surrounding strategic situation:</p><blockquote><p>10.3.2.3 Motivations that <i>donâ€™t</i> lead to instrumental convergence</p><p>Instrumental convergence is not inevitable in every possible motivation. It comes from AIs having preferences about the state of the world in the futureâ€”and most centrally, the distant future (say, weeks or months or longer)â€”such that investments in future options have time to pay themselves back. These are called â€œconsequentialistâ€ preferences.</p><p>Given that, one extreme position is to say â€œwell OK, if â€˜AGIs with consequentialist preferencesâ€™ leads to bad and dangerous things, then fine, weâ€™ll build AGIs without consequentialist preferences!â€. Alas, I donâ€™t think thatâ€™s a helpful plan. The minor problem is that we could <i>try</i> to ensure that the AGI preferences are not consequentialist, but fail, because technical alignment is hard. The much bigger problem is that, even if we succeed, the AGI wonâ€™t do anything of note, and will be hopelessly outcompeted and outclassed when the next company down the street, with a more cavalier attitude about safety, makes an AGI that <i>does</i> have consequentialist preferences. After all, itâ€™s hard to get things done except by wanting things to get done. For more on both these points, see my post <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/D4gEDdqWrgDPMtasc/thoughts-on-process-based-supervision-mona">Thoughts on â€œProcess-Based Supervisionâ€ /&nbsp;MONA (2023), Â§5.2â€“5.3</a>.</p><p>Then the opposite extreme position is to say â€œThatâ€™s right! If AGI is powerful at all, it will certainly have <i>exclusively</i> consequentialist preferences. So we should expect instrumental convergence to bite maximally hard.â€ But I donâ€™t think thatâ€™s right eitherâ€”see my post <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/KDMLJEXTWtkZWheXt/consequentialism-and-corrigibility">Consequentialism &amp; corrigibility (2021)</a>. Humans have a mix of consequentialist and non-consequentialist preferences, but societies of humans were nevertheless capable of developing language, science, and the global economy from scratch. As far as I know, brain-like AGI could be like that too. But we need to figure out exactly how.</p></blockquote><h3><i>What about RL today?</i></h3><p>I added a discussion of how I can say all this stuff about how RL agents are scary and we donâ€™t know how to control them â€¦ and yet, RL research seems to be going fine today! How do I reconcile that?</p><blockquote><p>10.3.3.1 â€œThe usual agent debugging loopâ€, and its future catastrophic breakdown</p><p>I was talking about the ubiquity of Goodhartâ€™s Law just above. You might be wondering: â€œIf Goodhartâ€™s Law is such an intractable problem, why are there thousands of impressive RL results on arXiv right now?â€ The answer is â€œthe usual agent debugging loopâ€. Itâ€™s applicable to any system involving RL, model-based planning, or both. Here it is:</p><ul><li>Step 1: Train the AI agent, and see what it does.</li><li>Step 2: If itâ€™s not doing what you had in mind, then turn it off, change something about the reward function or training environment, etc., and then try again.</li></ul><p>For example, if&nbsp;<a href="https://deepmind.google/discover/blog/specification-gaming-the-flip-side-of-ai-ingenuity/#:~:text=same%20green%20blocks-,over%20and%20over%20again,-.">the Coast Runners boat is racking up points by spinning in circles while on fire</a>, but we wanted the boat to follow the normal race course, then OK maybe letâ€™s try editing the reward function to incorporate waypoints, or letâ€™s delete the green blocks from the environment, etc.</p><p>Thatâ€™s a great approach for today, and it will continue being a great approach for a while. But eventually it will start failing in a catastrophic and irreversible wayâ€”and this is where instrumental convergence comes in.</p><p>The problem is: it will eventually become possible to train an AI that is&nbsp;<i>so good</i>&nbsp;at real-world planning, that it can make plans that are resilient to potential problemsâ€”and if the programmers are inclined to shut down or edit the AI under certain conditions, then thatâ€™s just another potential problem that the AI will incorporate into its planning process!</p><p>â€¦So if a sufficiently competent AI is trying to do something the programmers didnâ€™t want, the normal strategy of â€œjust turn off the AI, or edit it, to try to fix the problemâ€ stops working. The AI will&nbsp;<i>anticipate</i>&nbsp;that this programmer intervention is a possible obstacle to what itâ€™s trying to do, and make a plan resilient to that possible obstacle. This is no different than any other aspect of skillful planningâ€”if you expect that the cafeteria might be closed today, then youâ€™ll pack a bag lunch.</p><p>In the case at hand, a â€œresilientâ€ plan might look like the programmers not realizing that anything has gone wrong with the AI, because the AI is being deceptive about its plans and intentions. And meanwhile, the AI is gathering resources and exfiltrating itself so that it canâ€™t be straightforwardly turned off or edited, etc.</p><p>The upshot is: RL researchers today, and brain-like AGI researchers in the future, will be able to generate more and more impressive demos, and get more and more profits, for quite a while, even if neither they nor anyone else makes meaningful progress on this technical alignment problem. But that would only be up to a certain level of capability. Then it would&nbsp;flip sharply&nbsp;into being an existential threat.</p></blockquote><h3><i>What do I mean by (technical) alignment?</i></h3><p>Posts 10 &amp; 11 now have a clearer discussion of what I mean by (technical) alignment:</p><blockquote><p>10.1.1 What does â€œalignmentâ€ even mean?</p><p>The humans programming AGI will have some ideas about what they want that AGIâ€™s motivations to be. I define â€œthe technical alignment problemâ€ as the challenge of building AGI that actually has those motivations, via appropriate code, training environments, and so on.</p><p>This definition is deliberately agnostic about what motivations the AGI designers are going for.</p><p>Other researchers are not agnostic, but rather think that the â€œcorrectâ€ design intentions (for an AGIâ€™s motivation) are obvious, and they define the word â€œalignmentâ€ accordingly. Three common examples are</p><ul><li>(1) â€œI am designing the AGI so that, at any given point in time, itâ€™s trying to do what its human supervisor wants it to be trying to do right now.â€<ul><li>This AGI would be â€œalignedâ€ to the supervisorâ€™s intentions; I might refer to this vision as <a href="https://ai-alignment.com/corrigibility-3039e668638">â€œthe Paul Christiano version of corrigibilityâ€</a>.</li></ul></li><li>(2) â€œI am designing the AGI so that it shares the values of its human supervisor.<ul><li>This AGI would be â€œalignedâ€ to the supervisor; I might refer to this vision as&nbsp;<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/SvuLhtREMy8wRBzpC/ambitious-vs-narrow-value-learning">â€œambitious value learningâ€</a>.</li></ul></li><li>(3) â€œI am designing the AGI so that it shares the collective values of humanity.â€<ul><li>This AGI would be â€œalignedâ€ to humanity; I might refer to this vision as&nbsp;<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/w/coherent-extrapolated-volition">â€œCoherent Extrapolated Volitionâ€</a>.</li></ul></li></ul><p>Iâ€™m using â€œalignmentâ€ in this agnostic way because I think that the â€œcorrectâ€ intended AGI motivation is still an open question.</p><p>For example, maybe it will be possible to build an AGI that really just wants to do a specific, predetermined, narrow task (e.g. design a better solar cell), in a way that doesnâ€™t involve taking over the world etc. Such an AGI would not be â€œalignedâ€ to anything in particular, except for the original design intention. But I still want to use the term â€œalignedâ€ when talking about such an AGI.</p><p>â€¦</p><ul><li><strong>â€œAGI alignmentâ€</strong> (<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/wucncPjud27mLWZzQ/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-10-the-alignment-problem">previous post</a>) means that an AGIâ€™s motivations are what the AGI designers had in mind.&nbsp;This notion only makes sense for algorithms that have â€œmotivationsâ€ in the first place. What does that mean&nbsp;<i>in general</i>? Hoo boy, thatâ€™s&nbsp;<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/s/o58ZMNaovdztbLfvN"><u>a whole can of worms</u></a>. Is a sorting algorithm â€œmoitvatedâ€ to sort numbers? Or is it merely sorting them?? Letâ€™s not go there!&nbsp;For this series, itâ€™s easy. The â€œbrain-like AGIsâ€ that Iâ€™m talking about can definitely â€œwantâ€, and â€œtryâ€, and â€œbe motivatedâ€ to do things, in exactly the same common-sense way that a human can â€œwantâ€ to get out of debt.</li><li><strong>â€œAGI safetyâ€</strong> (<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/4basF9w9jaPZpoC8R/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-1-what-s-the-problem-and-why"><u>Post #1</u></a>) is about what the AGI&nbsp;<i>actually does</i>, not what itâ€™s&nbsp;<i>trying</i> to do. AGI safety means that the AGIâ€™s&nbsp;<i>actual behavior</i> does not lead to a â€œcatastrophic accidentâ€, as judged by the AGIâ€™s own designers.</li></ul></blockquote><h2><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/Sd4QvG4ZyjynZuHGt/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-12-two-paths-forward">Post 12: Two paths forward: â€œControlled AGIâ€ and â€œSocial-instinct AGIâ€</a></h2><h3><i>What exactly is the RL training environment?</i></h3><p>I added a subsection clarifying that, contrary to normal RL, we get to choose the source code for AGI but we donâ€™t really get to choose the training environment:</p><blockquote><p>12.5.2 The uncontrollable real world is part of the â€œtraining environmentâ€ too</p><p>RL practitioners are used to the idea that they get to freely choose the training environment. But for brain-like AGI, that frame is misleading, because of continual learning (<a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/fDPsYdDtkzhBp9A8D/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-8-takeaways-from-neuro-1-2-on#8_2_2_Continual_learning_implies_no_fundamental_training_versus_deployment_distinction">Â§8.2.2</a>). The programmers get to choose the â€œchildhood environmentâ€, so to speak, but sooner or later, brain-like AGI will wind up in the real world, doing what it thinks is best by its own lights.</p><p>By analogy, generations of parents have tried to sculpt their childrenâ€™s behavior, and theyâ€™re often successful, as long as the kid is still living with them and under their close supervision. Then the children become adults, living for years in a distant city, and the children often find that very different behaviors and beliefs fit them better, whether the parents like it or not.</p><p>Again see <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/aodPs8H9dQxpXAcwk/heritability-behaviorism-and-within-lifetime-rl">â€œHeritability, Behaviorism, and Within-Lifetime RLâ€ (2023)</a> for further discussion of this point, plus <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/xXtDCeYLBR88QWebJ/heritability-five-battles#2__Maybe_you_care_about_heritability_because__you_re_trying_to_figure_out_whether_some_parenting_or_societal_intervention_will_have_a_desired_effect">â€œHeritability: Five Battlesâ€ (2025), Â§2</a> for nitpicky details and caveats.</p><p>By the same token, programmers can build a â€œchildhood environmentâ€ for their baby brain-like AGIs to hang out and grow up in, but sooner or later weâ€™ll need an AGI that <i>remains</i> aligned while roaming free in the real world. If we canâ€™t do that, careless people will still make brain-like AGIs that roam free in the real world, but they will all be misaligned. Thatâ€™s bad.</p><p>And while we can design the childhood environment, we canâ€™t control the real world. It is what it is.</p><p>Indeed, I think a solid starting-point mental model is that, if we want a brain-like AGI thatâ€™s aligned after being in the real world for a long time, then we should mostly forget about the childhood environment. Thanks to continual learning, the AGI will settle upon patterns of thought and behavior that it finds most suitable in the real world, which depends on its innate disposition (e.g. reward function), and on the details of the real world, but not on its childhood environment.</p><p>12.5.3 But â€œchildhood environmentâ€ does matter</p><p>â€¦</p></blockquote><h2><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/tj8AC3vhTnBywdZoA/intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety-15-conclusion-open-problems-1">Post 15: Conclusion: Open problems, how to help, AMA</a></h2><h3><i>â€œReward Function Designâ€</i></h3><p>I added the RL subfield of â€œreward function designâ€ as an 8th concrete research program that I endorse people working on:</p><blockquote><p>15.2.2.3 The <i>â€œreward function designâ€</i> research program â€” â­â­â­â­</p><p>See my post <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/oxvnREntu82tffkYW/we-need-a-field-of-reward-function-design">â€œWe need a field of Reward Function Designâ€ (2025)</a>. In brief: If people make brain-like AGI, or really any other kind of RL agent AGI, then the reward function is an especially important design degree of freedom for alignment. Alas, the field of RL has always been much more interested in how to maximize reward functions, than in what the reward function should be. The tiny field of reward function design contains a few interesting ideas, including curiosity drive, reward shaping, and inverse reinforcement learning. Letâ€™s find dozens more ideas in the same category!</p><p>This research program should hopefully link up with and complement â€œreverse-engineering human social instinctsâ€ (Â§15.2.1.2 above), as I believe that the AI field of reward function design, in its current primitive state, lacks the theoretical language to explain how human social instincts manage to have the properties that they have.</p><p>(See my post <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/xw8P8H4TRaTQHJnoP/reward-function-design-a-starter-pack">Reward Function Design: a starter pack (2025)</a> for a snapshot of my own thinking on this topic.)</p><p>I give this research program a priority score of&nbsp;<strong>4 stars out of 5</strong>. Or up it to 5 stars out of 5 when itâ€™s more narrowly targeted at <i>alignment-relevant</i> reward function design ideas.</p></blockquote><h1>Conclusion</h1><p>Those were just highlights; there were many other small changes and corrections. The <a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/s/HzcM2dkCq7fwXBej8">blog version</a> has more detailed changelogs after each post. Happy for any feedback, either as blog comments, or by email, DM, etc.!</p><br /><br /><a href="https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/rreDwHXgnhEDKxkro/new-version-of-intro-to-brain-like-agi-safety#comments">Discuss</a>

### [Does Pentagon Pizza Theory Work?](https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/Li3Aw7sDLXTCcQHZM/does-pentagon-pizza-theory-work)

**æ¥æº**: LessWrong - AI

**æ‘˜è¦**: Published on January 22, 2026 7:24 PM GMT<br /><br /><p>As soon as modern data analysis became a thing, the US government has had to deal with people trying to use open source data to uncover its secrets.</p><p>During the early Cold War days and Americaâ€™s hydrogen bomb testing, there was an enormous amount of speculation about how the bombs actually worked. All nuclear technology involves refinement and purification of large amounts of raw substances into chemically pure substances. <a href="https://economics.ucla.edu/person/armen-alchian/">Armen Alchian</a> was an economist working at RAND and reasoned that any US company working in such raw materials and supplying the government would have made a killing leading up to the tests.</p><p>After checking financial data that RAND maintained on such companies, Alchian deduced that the secret sauce in the early fusion bombs was lithium and the Lithium Corporation of America was supplying the USG. The companyâ€™s stock had skyrocketed leading up to the <a href="https://nsarchive.gwu.edu/briefing-book/nuclear-vault/2024-02-29/castle-bravo-70-worst-nuclear-test-us-history">Castle Bravo</a> test either by way of enormous unexpected revenue gains from government contracts, or more amusingly, maybe by government insiders buying up the stock trying to make a mushroom-cloud-sized fortune with the knowledge that lithium was the key ingredient.</p><p>When word of this work got out, this story naturally ends with the FBI coming in and confiscating Alchianâ€™s data-driven research and the G-men giving him a stern lecture on national security, but he had just invented the first <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Event_study">event study</a> of the modern world.</p><h1>Pizza is the new lithium</h1><p>As you might have guessed, Alchianâ€™s intellectual heir is the X account <a href="https://x.com/PenPizzaReport">Pentagon Pizza Report</a>, which tracks activity in pizzerias with proximity to the Pentagon as reported by Google. Started in 2024 and with over 300K followers, it may be the gold-standard OSINT meme account. <a href="https://www.pizzint.watch/">Polymarket has even formalized it</a>.</p><p>Before the X account, I had never heard of the pentagon pizza theory, but it has its own <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentagon_pizza_theory">wikipedia page</a> and amazingly, this idea goes all the way back to the 1990s. The chain of causation is clear. People only work late in the Pentagon if thereâ€™s a lot of military work to do. When there is a lot of work to do, they get hungry and order a bunch of pizza. The Pentagon is huge and might strain local pizza production capacity; this shows up in Googleâ€™s location/traffic data that the feed captures. The world is messy and pizza data is messier, but can someone predict large scale US military action from public data on pizzerias?</p><p><a href="https://www.newsweek.com/pentagon-pizza-monitor-appeared-predict-israel-attack-2085501">The Pentagon has apparently disputed</a> this notion:</p><blockquote><p>The Takeout, a food and culture site, reported in January that while there are a number of eateries in the Pentagonâ€”where almost 30,000 people work each day, according to Arlington National Cemetery Toursâ€”it doesnâ€™t have its own pizzeria.</p><p>However, a Pentagon spokesperson has denied this, telling <i>Newsweek</i>, â€œThere are many pizza options available inside the Pentagon, also sushi, sandwiches, donuts, coffee, etc.â€</p></blockquote><p>Weâ€™re going to backtest this idea in the rest of this poast and get to the bottom of this one.</p><h1>The Data</h1><p>The Twitter API used to be a cornucopia for interesting data projects. Now you have to pay a fortune for API access and itâ€™s legitimately terrible. I actually could not get anything to work using the API or even using standard scraping tech to get the Pentagon Pizza Reportâ€™s tweet history; Musk has shut a lot of that shit down. I couldnâ€™t even get ChatGPT, run by Muskâ€™s rival Sam Altman, to help me improve the scraping against Twitter because it thought that was unethical. I even DMâ€™ed the Pentagon Pizza account to see if he would share his data, but he did not get back to me.</p><p>I ended up getting Claude code to write me a sophisticated Selenium script that very slowly and annoyingly scrolled through the tweet history in a browser and recovered the data as it did so.</p><p>I ended up with 648 clean PPR tweets. Hereâ€™s a short excerpt.</p><figure class="image"><img src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/Li3Aw7sDLXTCcQHZM/bvfk6662rmtquapr3evz" /><figcaption>Fig 1. First few PPR tweets.</figcaption></figure><h1>The Backtest</h1><p>Using this history, I wanted to check out US military adventures of the past year to see if they can be observed in the Pizza Reportâ€™s data. I could have selected others, but these are the most prominent that overlap with the Pentagon Pizza Reportâ€™s history and for the purposes of this study I preregistered them. I havenâ€™t yet looked at any of the Pentagon Pizza Reportâ€™s data as of writing this section.</p><h2>Fordow bombing</h2><p>On June 22nd, 2025, Operation Midnight Hammer was a sophisticated collection of strikes targeting Iranian nuclear infrastructure. This involved a large military footprint and careful planning much of which doubtlessly took place with involvement from the Pentagon.</p><h2>Maduro capture</h2><p>On January 3rd, 2026, Operation Absolute Resolve was an operation to capture Venezuelaâ€™s leader Maduro and bring him to justice in the United States. Again, this involved sophisticated intelligence and military cooperation in the Caribbean.</p><h2>The Houthi stuff</h2><p>This oneâ€™s interesting and takes place over a longer time frame, March 15th going into May 2025. Operation Rough Rider consisted of strikes against Houthi positions obstructing shipping in the Red Sea. This choice potentially allows us to see any longer term pizza signal that exists in the X data.</p><p>Evaluating whether PPR activity is faithfully related to these three events is a complicated statistical problem. The PPR history is quite clearly not a random sampling or uniform record of pizza-related activity near the Pentagon; I couldnâ€™t confirm this with the user, but itâ€™s plausibly selecting to tweet during unusual pizza activity levels. Without knowing how it works, you canâ€™t make many sound statistical assumptions about it.</p><p>Letâ€™s look at a few basic series.</p><figure class="image"><img src="https://res.cloudinary.com/lesswrong-2-0/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto/v1/mirroredImages/Li3Aw7sDLXTCcQHZM/detrva7bni1jrokgu8ax" /><figcaption>Fig 2. Weekly rolling PPR tweet volume since August 2024, broken down by tweet type.</figcaption></figure><p>This dataset is a dogâ€™s breakfast. Look at that giant gap in the volume! This wonâ€™t be useful at all for examining the Houthi strike test in March 2025, so weâ€™re stuck with just the Maduro capture and the Fordow bombing. There is a pretty astonishing spike in tweet volumes leading up to the Fordow strikes. To my eye, this looks more like the hiatus ending than some sort of tweet storm preceding that, but weâ€™ll return to that. First off, and most importantly, WTF is â€œFreddieâ€™sâ€?</p><p><a href="https://www.freddiesbeachbar.com/">Freddieâ€™s Beach Bar and Restaurant </a>is a gay bar in Arlington near the Pentagon. It is obviously hilarious, and whatâ€™s interesting about the Freddie tweets is that the account author pretty clearly intends for this to act as a control for the pizza activity measure. If after hours military activity at the Pentagon is causing increased pizzeria patronage, Freddieâ€™s wonâ€™t be affected by that. But if some exogenous thing is causing increased activity for both nearby pizzerias and Freddieâ€™s, weâ€™d see that and know to discount how meaningful the pizza signal is.</p><p>So bravo to the PPR account for this little statistical improvement. This means that the most meaningful parts of Figure 2 will be when the yellow line and the blue line diverge.</p><p>I created a pair of slightly differently specified â€œFreddie-controlledâ€ pizza activity indices which track the divergence of the Pentagon Pizza market activity versus Freddieâ€™s activity. ### [Fragments: January 22](https://martinfowler.com/fragments/2026-01-22.html)

**æ¥æº**: Martin Fowler

**æ‘˜è¦**: <p>My colleagues here at Thoughtworks have announced <a href="https://www.thoughtworks.com/ai/works">AI/worksâ„¢</a>, a platform for our work using AI-enabled software development. <p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>Simon Couch <a href="https://www.simonpcouch.com/blog/2026-01-20-cc-impact/">examines the electricity consumption</a> of using AI. Heâ€™s a heavy user: â€œusually programming for a few hours, and driving 2 or 3 Claude Code instances at a timeâ€. He finds his usage of electricity is orders of magnitude more than typical estimates based on the â€œtypical queryâ€.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>On a median day, I estimate I consume 1,300 Wh through Claude Codeâ€”4,400 â€œtypical queriesâ€ worth.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>But itâ€™s still not a massive amount of power - similar to that of running a dishwasher.</p>

<p>A caveat to this is that this is â€œnapkin mathâ€ because we donâ€™t have decent data about how these models use resources. I agree with him that we ought to.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>My namesake Chad Fowler (no relation) considers that the movement to agentic coding creates a similar <a href="https://aicoding.leaflet.pub/3mbrvhyye4k2e">shift in rigor and discipline</a> as appeared in Extreme Programming, dynamic languages, and continuous deployment.</p>

<p>In Extreme Programmingâ€™s case, this meant a lot of discipline around testing, continuous integration, and keeping the code-base healthy. My current view is that with AI-enabled development we need to be rigorous about evaluating the software, both for its observable behavior and its internal quality.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>The engineers who thrive in this environment will be the ones who relocate discipline rather than abandon it. Theyâ€™ll treat generation as a capability that demands more precision in specification, not less. Theyâ€™ll build evaluation systems that are harder to fool than the ones they replaced. Theyâ€™ll refuse the temptation to mistake velocity for progress.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>Thereâ€™s been much written about the dreadful events in Minnesota, and Iâ€™ve not felt Iâ€™ve had anything useful to add to them. <blockquote>
  <p>Is this America now? A country where unaccountable and poorly trained government agents go door to door, arresting and beating people on pure suspicion, and shooting people who donâ€™t obey their every order or who try to get away? â€œWhen a federal officer gives you instructions, you abide by them and then you get to keep your lifeâ€ is a perfect description of an authoritarian police state. <p>My worries about these kinds of developments were what animated me to urge against voting for Trump in the <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/vote-against-trump.html">2016 election</a>. But now those constitutional Republicans are absent or quiescent. I fear that what weâ€™ve seen in Minneapolis will be a harbinger of worse to come.</p>

<p>I also second John Gruberâ€™s <a href="https://daringfireball.net/2026/01/lets_call_a_murder_a_murder">praise of bystander Caitlin Callenson</a>:</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>But then, after the murderous agent fired three shotsâ€‰â€”â€‰just 30 or 40 feet in front of Callensonâ€‰â€”â€‰Callenson had the courage and conviction to stay with the scene and keep filming. <p>The recent activity in  Venezuala reminds me that Iâ€™ve long felt that Trump is a Hugo ChÃ¡vez figure - a charismatic populist whoâ€™s keen on wrecking institutions and norms. Trump is old, so wonâ€™t be with us for that much longer - but the question is: â€œwho is Trumpâ€™s Maduro?â€</p>

### [Conversation: LLMs and the what/how loop](https://martinfowler.com/articles/convo-what-how.html)

**æ¥æº**: Martin Fowler

**æ‘˜è¦**: <p>A conversation between <b class="author">Unmesh Joshi</b>, <b class="author">Rebecca
      Parsons</b>, and <b class="author">Martin Fowler</b> on how LLMs help us
      shape the abstractions in our software. We view our challenge as building
      systems that survive change, requiring us to manage our cognitive load. We
      can do this by mapping the &#x201c;what&#x201d; of we want our software to do into the
      &#x201c;how&#x201d; of programming languages. This &#x201c;what&#x201d; and &#x201c;how&#x201d; are built up in a
      feedback loop. TDD helps us operationalize that loop, and LLMs allow us to
      explore that loop in an informal and more fluid manner.</p>

<p><a class="more" href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/convo-what-how.html">moreâ€¦</a></p>

### [Stop Picking Sides: Manage the Tension Between Adaptation and                Optimization](https://martinfowler.com/articles/stop-picking-sides.html)

**æ¥æº**: Martin Fowler

**æ‘˜è¦**: <div class="img-link"><a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/stop-picking-sides.html"><img src="https://martinfowler.com/articles/stop-picking-sides/infograph.jpg" width="" /></a></div>

<p><b class="author">Jim Highsmith</b> notes that many teams have turned into
      tribes wedded to exclusively adaptation or optimization. But he feels this
      misses the point that both of these are important, and we need to manage
      the tension between them. We can do this by thinking of two operating
      modes: explore (adaptation-dominant) and exploit (optimization dominant).
      We tailor a team's operating model to a particular blend of the two -
      considering uncertainty, risk, cost of change, and an evidence threshold.
      We should be particularly careful at the points where there is a handoff
      between the two modes</p>

<p><a class="more" href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/stop-picking-sides.html">moreâ€¦</a></p>

### [Fragments: January  8](https://martinfowler.com/fragments/2026-01-08.html)

**æ¥æº**: Martin Fowler

**æ‘˜è¦**: <p>Anthropic report on <a href="https://www.anthropic.com/research/how-ai-is-transforming-work-at-anthropic">how their AI is changing their own software development practice</a>.</p>

<ul>
  <li>Most usage is for debugging and helping understand existing code</li>
  <li>Notable increase in using it for implementing new features</li>
  <li>Developers using it for 59% of their work and getting 50% productivity increase</li>
  <li>14% of developers are â€œpower usersâ€ reporting much greater gains</li>
  <li>Claude helps developers to work outside their core area</li>
  <li>Concerns about changes to the profession, career evolution, and social dynamics</li>
</ul>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>Much of the discussion about using LLMs for software development lacks details on workflow. Rather than just hear people gush about how wonderful it is, I want to understand the gritty details. What kinds of interactions occur with the LLM? What decisions do the humans make? When reviewing LLM outputs, what kinds of things are the humans looking for, what corrections do they make?</p>

<p><a href="https://obie.medium.com/what-used-to-take-months-now-takes-days-cc8883cc21e9">Obie Fernandez</a> has written a post that goes into these kinds of details. Over the Christmas / New Year period he used Claude to build a knowledge distillation application, that takes transcripts from Claude Code sessions, slack discussion, github PR threads etc, turns them into an RDF graph database, and provides a web app with natural language ways to query them.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>Not a proof of concept. Not a demo. The first cut of Nexus, a production-ready system with authentication, semantic search, an MCP server for agent access, webhook integrations for our primary SaaS platforms, comprehensive test coverage, deployed, integrated and ready for full-scale adoption at my company this coming Monday. Nearly 13,000 lines of code.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>The article is long, but worth the time to read it.</p>

<p>An important feature of his workflow is relying on <a href="https://martinfowler.com/bliki/TestDrivenDevelopment.html">Test-Driven Development</a></p>

<blockquote>
  <p>Hereâ€™s what made this sustainable rather than chaotic: TDD. Test-driven development. For most of the features, I insisted that Claude Code follow the red-green-refactor cycle with me. Write a failing test first. Make it pass with the simplest implementation. Then refactor while keeping tests green.</p>

  <p>This wasnâ€™t just methodology purism. TDD served a critical function in AI-assisted development: it kept me in the loop. When youâ€™re directing thousands of lines of code generation, you need a forcing function that makes you actually understand whatâ€™s being built. Tests are that forcing function. You canâ€™t write a meaningful test for something you donâ€™t understand. And you canâ€™t verify that a test correctly captures intent without understanding the intent yourself.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>The account includes a major refactoring, and much evolution of the initial version of the tool. Itâ€™s also an interesting glimpse of how AI tooling may finally make RDF useful.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>When thinking about requirements for software, most discussions focus on prioritization. Some folks talk about buckets such as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MoSCoW_method">MoSCoW set</a>: Must, Should, Could, and Want. (The old joke being that, in MoSCoW, the cow is silent, because hardly any requirements end up in those buckets.) <a href="https://world.hey.com/jason/the-obvious-the-easy-and-the-possible-2e11a3fb">Jason Fried</a> has a different set of buckets for interface design: <strong>Obvious, Easy, and Possible</strong>. This immediately resonates with me: a good way of think about how to allocate the cognitive costs for those who use a tool.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p><a href="https://www.platformer.news/fake-uber-eats-whisleblower-hoax-debunked/">Casey Newton</a> explains how he followed up on an interesting story of dark patterns in food delivery, and found it to be a fake story, buttressed by AI image and document creation. On one hand, it clarifies the important role reporters play in exposing lies that get traction on the internet. But time taken to do this is time not spent on investigating real stories</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>For most of my career up until this point, the document shared with me by the whistleblower would have seemed highly credible in large part because it would have taken so long to put together. Who would take the time to put together a detailed, 18-page technical document about market dynamics just to troll a reporter? Who would go to the trouble of creating a fake badge?</p>

  <p>Today, though, the report can be generated within minutes, and the badge within seconds. And while no good reporter would ever have published a story based on a single document and an unknown source, plenty would take the time to investigate the documentâ€™s contents and see whether human sources would back it up.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>The internet has always been full of slop, and we have always needed to be wary of what we read there. AI now makes it easy to manufacture convincing looking evidence, and this is never more dangerous than when it confirms strongly held beliefs and fears.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p><a href="https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7413956151144542208/">Kent Beck</a>:</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>The descriptions of Spec-Driven development that I have seen emphasize writing the whole specification before implementation. This encodes the (to me bizarre) assumption that you arenâ€™t going to learn anything during implementation that would change the specification.
Iâ€™ve heard this story so many times told so many ways by well-meaning folksâ€“if only we could get the specification â€œrightâ€, the rest of this would be easy.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>Like him, that story has been the constant background siren to my career in tech. But the <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/llm-learning-loop.html">learning loop</a> of experimentation is essential to the model building thatâ€™s at the heart of any kind of worthwhile specification. As <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/llm-learning-loop.html">Unmesh puts it:</a></p>

<blockquote>
  <p>Large Language Models give us great leverageâ€”but they only work if we focus on learning and understanding. They make it easier to explore ideas, to set things up, to translate intent into code across many specialized languages. But the real capabilityâ€”our ability to respond to changeâ€”comes not from how fast we can produce code, but from how deeply we understand the system we are shaping.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>When Kent defined Extreme Programming, he made <em>feedback</em> one of its four core values. It strikes me that the key to making the full use of AI in software development is how to use it to accelerate the feedback loops.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>As I listen to people who are serious with AI-assisted programming, the crucial thing I hear is managing context. Programming-oriented tools are geting more sophisticated for that, but thereâ€™s also efforts at providing simpler tools, that allow customization. <a href="https://lixo.org">Carlos Villela</a> recently recommended Pi, and its developer, <a href="https://mariozechner.at/posts/2025-11-30-pi-coding-agent/">Mario Zechner, has an interesting blog</a> on its development.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>So whatâ€™s an old guy yelling at Claudes going to do? Heâ€™s going to write his own coding agent harness and give it a name thatâ€™s entirely un-Google-able, so there will never be any users. Which means there will also never be any issues on the GitHub issue tracker. How hard can it be?</p>
</blockquote>

<p>If I ever get the time to sit and really play with these tools, then something like Pi would be something Iâ€™d like to try out. Although as an addict to The One True Editor, Iâ€™m interested in some of libraries that work with that, such as <a href="https://github.com/karthink/gptel">gptel</a>. That would enable me to use Emacsâ€™s inherent programability to create my own command set to drive the interaction with LLMs.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>Outside of my professional work, Iâ€™ve <a href="https://boardgamegeek.com/blog/13064/martins-7th-decade">posting regularly about my boardgaming</a> on the specialist site BoardGameGeek. However its blogging environment doesnâ€™t do a good job of providing an index to my posts, so Iâ€™ve created <a href="https://martinfowler.com/boardgames/">a list of my BGG posts </a> on my own site. If youâ€™re interested in my regular posts on boardgaming, and youâ€™re on BGG you can subscribe to me there. If youâ€™re not on BGG you can  subscribe to the blogâ€™s <a href="https://boardgamegeek.com/rss/blog/13064">RSS feed</a>.</p>

<p>Iâ€™ve also created a list of <a href="https://martinfowler.com/boardgames/fav-games.html">my favorite board games</a>.</p>

### [Fragments: December 16](https://martinfowler.com/fragments/2025-12-16.html)

**æ¥æº**: Martin Fowler

**æ‘˜è¦**: <p><a href="https://www.linkedin.com/in/gitanjalivenkatraman/">Gitanjali Venkatraman</a> does wonderful illustrations of complex subjects (which is why I was so happy to work with her on our <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/expert-generalist.html">Expert Generalists</a> article). She has now published the latest in her series of illustrated guides: tackling the complex topic of <a href="https://www.thoughtworks.com/content/dam/thoughtworks/documents/blog/mainframe_modernisation_illustrated_guide_2025.pdf">Mainframe Modernization</a></p>

<p><a href="https://www.thoughtworks.com/content/dam/thoughtworks/documents/blog/mainframe_modernisation_illustrated_guide_2025.pdf"><img alt="" src="https://martinfowler.com/fragments/2025/gitanjali-mainframe.png" /></a></p>

<p>In it she illustrates the history and value of mainframes, why modernization is so tricky, and how to tackle the problem by breaking it down into tractable pieces. I love the clarity of her explanations, and smile frequently at her way of enhancing her words with her quirky pictures.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>Gergely Orosz on <a href="https://bsky.app/profile/gergely.pragmaticengineer.com/post/3m7xd33hi5c2d">social media</a></p>

<blockquote>
  <p>Unpopular opinion:</p>

  <p>Current code review tools just donâ€™t make much sense for AI-generated code</p>

  <p>When reviewing code I really want to know:</p>

  <ul>
    <li>The prompt made by the dev</li>
    <li>What corrections the other dev made to the code</li>
    <li>Clear marking of code AI-generated not changed by a human</li>
  </ul>
</blockquote>

<p>Some people pushed back saying they donâ€™t (and shouldnâ€™t care) whether it was written by a human, generated by an LLM, or copy-pasted from Stack Overflow.</p>

<p>In my view it matters <em>a lot</em> - because of the second vital purpose of code review.</p>

<p>When asked why do code reviews, most people will answer the first vital purpose - quality control. We want to ensure bad code gets blocked before it hits <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/branching-patterns.html#mainline">mainline</a>. We do this to avoid bugs and to avoid other quality issues, in particular comprehensibility and ease of change.</p>

<p>But I hear the second vital purpose less often: code review is a mechanism to communicate and educate. If Iâ€™m submitting some sub-standard code, and it gets rejected, I want to know why so that I can improve my programming. Maybe Iâ€™m unaware of some library features, or maybe thereâ€™s some project-specific standards I havenâ€™t run into yet, or maybe my naming isnâ€™t as clear as I thought it was. Whatever the reasons, I need to know in order to learn. And my employer needs me to learn, so I can be more effective.</p>

<p>We need to know the writer of the code we review both so we can communicate our better practice to them, but also to know how to improve things. With a human, its a conversation, and perhaps some documentation if we realize weâ€™ve needed to explain things repeatedly. But with an LLM itâ€™s about how to modify its context, as well as humans learning how to better drive the LLM.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p>Wondering why Iâ€™ve been making a lot of posts like this recently? <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/writing-fragments.html">I explain why</a> Iâ€™ve been reviving the link blog.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p><a href="https://simonwillison.net/2025/Dec/10/html-tools/">Simon Willison</a> describes how he uses LLMs to build disposable but useful web apps</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>These are the characteristics I have found to be most productive in building tools of this nature:</p>

  <ol>
    <li>A single file: inline JavaScript and CSS in a single HTML file means the least hassle in hosting or distributing them, and crucially means you can copy and paste them out of an LLM response.</li>
    <li>Avoid React, or anything with a build step. The problem with React is that JSX requires a build step, which makes everything massively less convenient. I prompt â€œno reactâ€ and skip that whole rabbit hole entirely.</li>
    <li>Load dependencies from a CDN. The fewer dependencies the better, but if thereâ€™s a well known library that helps solve a problem Iâ€™m happy to load it from CDNjs or jsdelivr or similar.</li>
    <li>Keep them small. A few hundred lines means the maintainability of the code doesnâ€™t matter too much: any good LLM can read them and understand what theyâ€™re doing, and rewriting them from scratch with help from an LLM takes just a few minutes.</li>
  </ol>
</blockquote>

<p>His repository includes all these tools, together with transcripts of the chats that got the LLMs to build them.</p>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p><a href="https://obie.medium.com/what-happens-when-the-coding-becomes-the-least-interesting-part-of-the-work-ab10c213c660">Obie Fernandez</a>: while many engineers are underwhelmed by AI tools, some senior engineers are finding them really valuable. He feels that senior engineers have an oft-unspoken mindset, which in conjunction with an LLM, enables the LLM to be much more valuable.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>Levels of abstraction and generalization problems get talked about a lot because theyâ€™re easy to name. But theyâ€™re far from the whole story.</p>

  <p>Other tools show up just as often in real work:</p>

  <ul>
    <li>A sense for blast radius. Knowing which changes are safe to make loudly and which should be quiet and contained.</li>
    <li>A feel for sequencing. Knowing when a technically correct change is still wrong because the system or the team isnâ€™t ready for it yet.</li>
    <li>An instinct for reversibility. Preferring moves that keep options open, even if they look less elegant in the moment.</li>
    <li>An awareness of social cost. Recognizing when a clever solution will confuse more people than it helps.</li>
    <li>An allergy to false confidence. Spotting places where tests are green but the model is wrong.</li>
  </ul>
</blockquote>

<p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>

<p><a href="https://friendlybit.com/python/writing-justhtml-with-coding-agents/">Emil StenstrÃ¶m</a> built an HTML5 parser in python using coding agents, using Github Copilot in Agent mode with Claude Sonnet 3.7. He automatically approved most commands. It took him â€œa couple of months on off-hoursâ€, including at least one restart from scratch. The parser now passes all the tests in html5lib test suite.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>After writing the parser, I still donâ€™t know HTML5 properly. The agent wrote it for me. I guided it when it came to API design and corrected bad decisions at the high level, but it did ALL of the gruntwork and wrote all of the code.</p>

  <p>I handled all git commits myself, reviewing code as it went in. I didnâ€™t understand all the algorithmic choices, but I understood when it didnâ€™t do the right thing.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>Although he gives an overview of what happens, thereâ€™s not very much information on his workflow and how he interacted with the LLM. Thereâ€™s certainly not enough detail here to try to replicate his approach. This is contrast to Simon Willison (above) who has detailed links to his chat transcripts - although they are much smaller tools and I havenâ€™t looked at them properly to see how useful they are.</p>

<p>One thing that is clear, however, is the vital need for a comprehensive test suite. Much of his work is driven by having that suite as a clear guide for him and the LLM agents.</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>JustHTML is about 3,000 lines of Python with 8,500+ tests passing. I couldnâ€™t have written it this quickly without the agent.</p>

  <p>But â€œquicklyâ€ doesnâ€™t mean â€œwithout thinking.â€ I spent a lot of time reviewing code, making design decisions, and steering the agent in the right direction. The agent did the typing; I did the thinking.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â </p>

<p>Then Simon Willison <a href="https://simonwillison.net/2025/Dec/15/porting-justhtml/">ported the library to JavaScript</a>:</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>Time elapsed from project idea to finished library: about 4 hours, during which I also bought and decorated a Christmas tree with family and watched the latest Knives Out movie.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>One of his lessons:</p>

<blockquote>
  <p>If you can reduce a problem to a robust test suite you can set a coding agent loop loose on it with a high degree of confidence that it will eventually succeed. I called this <a href="https://simonwillison.net/2025/Sep/30/designing-agentic-loops/">designing the agentic loop</a> a few months ago. I think itâ€™s the key skill to unlocking the potential of LLMs for complex tasks.</p>
</blockquote>

<p>Our experience at Thoughtworks backs this up. Weâ€™ve been doing a fair bit of work recently in legacy modernization (mainframe and otherwise) using AI to migrate substantial software systems. Having a robust test suite is necessary (but not sufficient) to making this work. I hope to share my colleaguesâ€™ experiences on this in the coming months.</p>

<p>But before I leave Willisonâ€™s post, I should highlight his final open questions on the legalities, ethics, and effectiveness of all this - they are well-worth contemplating.</p>

### [Writing Fragments](https://martinfowler.com/articles/writing-fragments.html)

**æ¥æº**: Martin Fowler

**æ‘˜è¦**: <p>If youâ€™re a regular reader of my site, youâ€™ll have noticed that in the
last few months Iâ€™ve been making a <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/20251204-frags.html">number</a> of â€œfragmentsâ€ <a href="https://martinfowler.com/articles/2025-11-19-frags.html">posts</a>. Such a post
is a short post with a bunch of little, unconnected segments. These are
usually a reference to something Iâ€™ve found on the web, sometimes a small
thought of my own.</p>

<p>A few years ago, I wouldnâ€™t have covered these topics with posts on my
own site. Instead I would use Twitter, either retweeting someone elseâ€™s
point, or just highlighting something Iâ€™d found. But since the Muskover,
Twitter has effectively died. Iâ€™m not saying that due to any technical
issues with the site, which has mostly just been fine, nor directly due to
any of the policy changes there. The point is that lots of people have left, so that
the audience I would have reached with Twitter is now fragmented. Some
remain on X, but I see more activity on LinkedIn. Thereâ€™s also Fediverse/Mastodon
and Bluesky.</p>

<p>What this means for short posts is that I can no longer just post in one
place. When I announce new articles on martinfowler.com, I announce now on
four social media sites (X, LinkedIn, Fediverse, and Bluesky). It makes
sense to do this, but I donâ€™t want to go through all this hassle for the
kind of micro-post that Twitter served so well.</p>

<p>So Iâ€™ve started to batch them up. When I see something interesting, I
make a note. When I have enough notes, I post a fragments post. Initially I
did this in a rather ad-hoc way, just using the same mechanisms I use for
most articles, but last week I started to put in some more deliberate
mechanisms into the site. (If youâ€™re observant, youâ€™ll spot that in the URLs.)</p>

<p>One benefit of all of this, at least in my book, is that it means my material is
now fully visible in RSS. Iâ€™m probably showing my age, but Iâ€™m a big fan of RSS
(or in my case, strictly Atom) feeds. I miss the feel of the heyday of the
â€œblogosphereâ€ before it got steamrolled by social media, and these fragment
posts are, of course, just the same as the link blogs from that era. I still use my
RSS reader every day to keep up with writers I like. (Iâ€™m pleased that Substack
makes its content available via RSS.) It bothered me a bit that my micro-founts
of Twitter knowledge werenâ€™t visible on RSS, but was too lazy to do something
about it. Now I donâ€™t need to - the fragments are available in my RSS feed.</p>

**æ¥æº**: Martin Fowler

**æ‘˜è¦**: <p><a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2025/12/03/magazine/chatbot-writing-style.html?utm_source=substack&amp;utm_medium=email">Why does AI write likeâ€¦ that</a> (NYT, gift link). <p>Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â â„</p>
